❓:Let P in mathbb{K}[X] and (a, b) in mathbb{K}^{2} such that a neq b. Knowing that the remainder of the Euclidean division of P by X-a is 1 and that the remainder of the Euclidean division of P by X-b is -1, determine the remainder of the Euclidean division of P by (X-a)(X-b).

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I need to find the remainder when the polynomial P is divided by (X - a)(X - b), given that when P is divided by (X - a) the remainder is 1, and when divided by (X - b) the remainder is -1. Also, a is not equal to b. Hmm, let's think step by step.First, I remember that when you divide a polynomial by a linear factor like (X - a), the remainder is just a constant. That's the Remainder Theorem, right? So in this case, P(a) = 1 and P(b) = -1. That's given. Now, if we divide P by a quadratic polynomial (X - a)(X - b), the remainder should be a first-degree polynomial, because the degree of the remainder is less than the divisor. So the remainder R(X) would look like R(X) = cX + d, where c and d are constants from the field K. Our goal is to find c and d.Since when we divide P by (X - a)(X - b), we can write P(X) = (X - a)(X - b)Q(X) + R(X), where Q(X) is the quotient polynomial. Then, substituting X = a into this equation, we get P(a) = 0 + R(a) = c*a + d. But we know P(a) = 1, so c*a + d = 1. Similarly, substituting X = b gives P(b) = 0 + R(b) = c*b + d = -1.So now we have a system of two equations:1) c*a + d = 12) c*b + d = -1We need to solve for c and d. Let's subtract the second equation from the first to eliminate d:(c*a + d) - (c*b + d) = 1 - (-1)c*(a - b) = 2Since a ≠ b, we can divide both sides by (a - b):c = 2 / (a - b)Then plug this value of c back into one of the original equations to find d. So d = (-a - b)/(a - b) = -(a + b)/(a - b)So R(X) is (2/(a - b))X - (a + b)/(a - b). To check, maybe substitute back into the original equations.For X = a:R(a) = (2a)/(a - b) - (a + b)/(a - b) = [2a - a - b]/(a - b) = (a - b)/(a - b) = 1, which matches P(a) = 1.For X = b:R(b) = (2b)/(a - b) - (a + b)/(a - b) = [2b - a - b]/(a - b) = (b - a)/(a - b) = - (a - b)/(a - b) = -1, which matches P(b) = -1.So the remainder is correct.Alternatively, maybe we can express R(X) in another form. Let's see:R(X) = [2X - (a + b)] / (a - b). If we factor numerator and denominator, maybe notice that 2X - a - b can be written as (X - a) + (X - b). Let's check:(X - a) + (X - b) = 2X - a - b. Exactly. So,R(X) = ( (X - a) + (X - b) ) / (a - b )But (a - b) is the same as -(b - a). So,R(X) = - ( (X - a) + (X - b) ) / (b - a )But not sure if that helps. Alternatively, maybe leave it as R(X) = [2/(a - b)]X - (a + b)/(a - b). Either way, that's the remainder.Alternatively, perhaps write R(X) as (2X - a - b)/(a - b). Since division by a scalar is allowed here.But in polynomial terms, the coefficients are 2/(a - b) for X and - (a + b)/(a - b) for the constant term. So the answer should be presented as a first-degree polynomial with these coefficients.Alternatively, since the problem is in K[X], and a and b are elements of K, then (a - b) is invertible because a ≠ b. So the coefficients are well-defined in the field K.Therefore, the remainder is R(X) = [2/(a - b)]X - (a + b)/(a - b)Alternatively, factor 1/(a - b):R(X) = (2X - a - b)/(a - b)But maybe written as:R(X) = frac{2}{a - b}X - frac{a + b}{a - b}Yes, that's the standard form.So, summarizing the steps:1. Recognize that remainder upon division by (X - a)(X - b) is linear: R(X) = cX + d.2. We need to determine the remainder of the Euclidean division of ( P ) by ( (X - a)(X - b) ).1. Remainder Theorem Application: Since ( P(a) = 1 ) and ( P(b) = -1 ), we use these values to set up our equations.2. 🔑:The translation of the hypotheses allows us to deduce the existence of polynomials Q_{1} and Q_{2} satisfying the following system:left{begin{array} { l } { P = ( X - a ) Q _ { 1 } + 1 } { P = ( X - b ) Q _ { 2 } - 1 }end{array} quad text { hence } quad left{begin{array}{l}P(a)=1 P(b)=-1end{array}right.right.Let's determine the remainder of the Euclidean division of P by (X-a)(X-b):P=(X-a)(X-b) Q_{3}+c X+dwhere Q_{3} is a certain polynomial (which we do not wish to determine), and c, d are constants to be determined.Evaluate (star) at a and b to write the following system:left{begin{array} { l } { P ( a ) = c a + d } { P ( b ) = c b + d }end{array} Longleftrightarrow left{begin{array} { r l } { 1 = c a + d } { - 1 = c b + d } & { L _ { 2 } }end{array} Longleftrightarrow underset { L _ { 2 } - L _ { 1 } } { } left{begin{array} { r l } { 1 = c a + d } { - 2 = } & { c ( b - a ) }end{array} underset { a neq b } { Longleftrightarrow } left{begin{array}{l}d=frac{b+a}{b-a} c=frac{2}{a-b}end{array}right.right.right.right.The remainder of the Euclidean division of P by (X-a)(X-b) is given by:R(X)=c X+d=frac{2}{a-b} X+frac{b+a}{b-a}## Roots## Link between roots and divisibility& Explanation & We recall that a root lambda in mathbb{K} of P is a value that nullifies P, i.e., satisfies P(lambda)=0. It is important to pay attention to the set in which we are looking for the roots, as shown by the following concrete examples.Example 2 X^{2}-2 has two real roots pm sqrt{2}, but has no rational roots (since sqrt{2} is irrational). X^{2}+1 has no roots in mathbb{R}, but has two complex roots pm i.& Explanation The following fundamental result allows us to link two distinct worlds (that of evaluation) and that of arithmetic (in terms of divisibility):## Proposition 1.2.10.Let lambda in mathbb{K} and P in mathbb{K}[X]. Then:begin{gathered}lambda text { is a root of } P(text { i.e. } P(lambda)=0) Longleftrightarrow P text { is divisible by }(X-lambda) text { (i.e. } exists Q in mathbb{K}[X], P=(X-lambda) Q)end{gathered}## Proof.Leftrightarrow The right-to-left direction is trivial.Rightarrow We perform the Euclidean division of P by (X-lambda). Evaluating at lambda then allows us to show that its remainder is zero.## Maximum number of roots& Explanation A polynomial cannot have more roots than its degree:## Theorem 1.2.11.Let n in mathbb{N}. A polynomial in mathbb{K}[X] of degree n has at most n distinct roots in mathbb{K}.Proof. Induction on n done in class.Example 3 There exist polynomials that have no real roots, for example:P(x)=x^{4}+1=left(x^{2}right)^{2}+1^{2}+2 x^{2}-2 x^{2}=left(x^{2}+1right)^{2}-(sqrt{2} x)^{2}=left(x^{2}+sqrt{2} x+1right)left(x^{2}-sqrt{2} x+1right)then we show that the discriminants of these quadratic polynomials are strictly negative (if necessary, we can refer to the section on quadratic polynomial reminders).Example 4 A polynomial P in mathbb{R}[X] of odd degree has at least one real root. It suffices to look at the limits at pm infty and conclude by the intermediate value theorem.## Corollary 1.2.12.- If a polynomial P has an infinite number of roots, THEN P is zero.- If a polynomial P of degree leqslant n has n+1 distinct roots, THEN P is zero.- IF P=sum_{k=0}^{n} a_{k} x^{k} is of degree n and has n distinct roots lambda_{1}, cdots, lambda_{n}, THEN:P(x)=a_{n}left(x-lambda_{1}right) cdotsleft(x-lambda_{n}right)Proof. The first two points directly follow from the previous theorem. The last point is proven in class by considering the difference.- Explanation Despite appearances, this corollary, though very intuitive, is one of the most important in this chapter! One of the recipes for solving polynomial exercises consists in:## INTERPRETING THE HYPOTHESES IN TERMS OF ROOTS OF A CERTAIN POLYNOMIAL

The goal is to flip 12 cards to face down, following the rule that a card can be flipped if the card two positions away (three steps apart in either direction) is still face up. The problem requires determining the number of different sequences of flips that achieve this.Key steps and reasoning:1. Understanding the Flipping Rule: A card can be flipped if at least one of the two cards three steps away (in either direction) is face up. This forms a dependency graph where each card is connected to the cards three steps away.2. Dependency Graph Structure: The dependency graph is a cycle where each card is connected to the next card three steps away. This cycle includes all 13 cards.3. Symmetry and Last Card: Each card is symmetric in the circle, so the number of sequences where any specific card is last is the same. Thus, the total number of sequences is 13 multiplied by the number of sequences for a fixed last card.4. Recursive Pattern and Small Cases: By analyzing smaller cases (n=3, n=4), it was observed that the number of sequences for a fixed last card follows the pattern (2^{n-2}). For example, for n=4, the number of sequences is (4 times 2^{4-2} = 16).5. General Formula: Extending this pattern to n=13, the number of sequences is (13 times 2^{13-2} = 13 times 2^{11}).6. Calculation: (2^{11} = 2048), leading to (13 times 2048 = 26624).Thus, the number of different sequences of flips Teacher D can use to achieve success is (boxed{26624}).]

🔑:【Answer】 26624【Solution】First, we rearrange all the cards with numbers to get the following figure, thus transforming the problem into: if there is a face-up card among adjacent cards, then Teacher D can flip the current card. Next, we will find that if there is a jump in the two card selections, it will inevitably result in at least 2 cards that cannot be flipped. As shown in the figure below, if the 4th card is flipped first, and then the 12th card is flipped. This divides the remaining cards into two segments, and each segment will inevitably leave at least one card that cannot be flipped, thus failing to meet the requirements of the problem.Up to this point, we know that there are 13 choices for the first card. Once the first card is chosen, the next cards to be flipped are all adjacent to this card. Each choice has two options: the adjacent card in a clockwise direction or the adjacent card in a counterclockwise direction. As long as 11 choices are made, one card will definitely be left, so there are a total of 13 times 2^{11}=26624 different card flipping sequences.

For ((A, B, C)) and ((b, a, c)) to form arithmetic sequences, we have (2B = A + C) and (2a = b + c). Given the congruences (A equiv a mod p), (B equiv b mod p), and (C equiv c mod p), we substitute (A = a + kp), (B = b + lp), and (C = c + mp) into the arithmetic sequence conditions. This leads to the equation (p(2l - k - m) = 2(a - b)). Since (p) is a prime and (a - b < p), (p) must divide 2, leading to (p = 2) or (p = 3). However, (p = 2) is invalid as it cannot satisfy (0 le b < a < c < p). Thus, (p = 3).For (p = 3), the only valid residues are (a = 1), (b = 0), and (c = 2). 🔑:From condition (d), we have (A,B,C)=(B-D,B,B+D) and (b,a,c)=(a-d,a,a+d). Condition text{(c)} states that pmid B-D-a, p | B-a+d, and pmid B+D-a-d. We subtract the first two to get pmid-d-D, and we do the same for the last two to get pmid 2d-D. We subtract these two to get pmid 3d. So pmid 3 or pmid d. The second case is clearly impossible, because that would make c=a+d>p, violating condition text{(b)}. So we have pmid 3, meaning p=3. Condition text{(b)} implies that (b,a,c)=(0,1,2) or (a,b,c)in (1,0,2)rightarrow (-2,0,2)text{ }(Dequiv 2text{ mod 3}). Now we return to condition text{(c)}, which now implies that (A,B,C)equiv(-2,0,2)pmod{3}. Now, we set B=3k for increasing positive integer values of k. B=0 yields no solutions. B=3 gives (A,B,C)=(1,3,5), giving us 1 solution. If B=6, we get 2 solutions, (4,6,8) and (1,6,11). Proceeding in the manner, we see that if B=48, we get 16 solutions. However, B=51 still gives 16 solutions because C_text{max}=2B-1=101>100. Likewise, B=54 gives 15 solutions. This continues until B=96 gives one solution. B=99 gives no solution. Thus, N=1+2+cdots+16+16+15+cdots+1=2cdotfrac{16(17)}{2}=16cdot 17=boxed{272}.

Therefore, this is the correct answer.So, the answer is boxed{336}.</think>To prove that the gnomes have at least 336 keys, we analyze the problem as follows:1. Problem Setup: There are 7 gnomes guarding 144 different locks (12 doors each with 12 locks). Any three gnomes together must have all 144 keys.2. Key Insight: If a gnome had fewer than 48 keys, there would be at least (144 - 47 = 97) keys missing. For any trio including this gnome, the remaining two gnomes must cover all 97 missing keys. However, if each gnome has at most 47 keys, the union of any two gnomes can cover at most (47 + 47 = 94) keys, which is insufficient to cover 97 keys. This contradiction implies each gnome must have at least 48 keys.3. Total Keys Calculation: With each gnome having at least 48 keys, the total number of keys across all 7 gnomes is at least (7 times 48 = 336).Thus, the total number of keys must be at least 336.[boxed{336}]]

🔑:## Solution.There must be 4 gnomes such that each of them has at least 48 keys (otherwise, 3 of them could be chosen in such a way that they collectively have fewer than 3 cdot 48=144 keys and therefore could not open the locks, contrary to the assumption). The other 3 gnomes collectively have at least 144 keys. Therefore, there are 4 gnomes with at least 48 keys each and 3 other gnomes with at least 144 keys, so the 7 gnomes have at least 4 cdot 48+144=336 keys.
